        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.4c04795"><meta property="og:title" content="Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/acsami.4c04795.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Nitrogen-doped carbon submicrospheres (NCSMs) are synthesized via an efficient and environmentally friendly one-pot polymerization reaction at room temperature, in which dopamine hydrochloride serves as the source for both carbon and nitrogen. Through leverage of its distinctive structure characterized by minimal surface area, fewer oxygen-containing functional groups, and a heightened presence of active nitrogen-doping sites, the synthesized NCSM showcases a noteworthy initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 84.8%, a remarkable sodium storage capacity of 384 mAh g–1, an impressive rate capability of 215 mAh g–1 at 10 A g–1, and a superior cyclic performance, maintaining 83.0% of its capacity after 2000 cycles. The submicron spherical structure, with its limited surface area and scarce oxygen-containing moieties, effectively curtails the irreversible sodium-ion loss in solid–electrolyte interphase film formation, resulting in heightened ICE. The abundant nitrogen doping can expand carbon-layer spacing as well as improve the electron/ion-transport dynamics, guaranteeing a high sodium storage capacity and a strong rate capability. Crucially, the synthesis method presented here is straightforward, effective, and amenable to scaling, offering a novel avenue for the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/acsami.4c04795.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931884475a0254b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931884475a0254b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '7c91b4b2-d3e6-4613-a9fe-e8d254976ce4'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

        <style>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931884475a0254b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931884475a0254b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c04795", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

        </div></div><div class="article__pubdate"><span>Energy, Environmental, and Catalysis Applications</span><time datetime="2024-05-23">May 23, 2024</time></div><h1 class="article_header-title" tabindex="0"><span class="hlFld-Title" property="headline">Low-Surface-Area Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Submicrospheres as High-Coulombic-Efficiency and High-Capacity Anodes for Practical Sodium-Ion Batteries</span><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy article link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Article link copied!</span></div></h1><ul class="loa"><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Hongwei Tao</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Hongwei Tao</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Institute of Smart City and Intelligent Transportation, Southwest Jiaotong University, Chengdu 611756, P. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/medium/am4c04795_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c04795&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Nitrogen-doped carbon submicrospheres (NCSMs) are synthesized via an efficient and environmentally friendly one-pot polymerization reaction at room temperature, in which dopamine hydrochloride serves as the source for both carbon and nitrogen. Through leverage of its distinctive structure characterized by minimal surface area, fewer oxygen-containing functional groups, and a heightened presence of active nitrogen-doping sites, the synthesized NCSM showcases a noteworthy initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 84.8%, a remarkable sodium storage capacity of 384 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, an impressive rate capability of 215 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and a superior cyclic performance, maintaining 83.0% of its capacity after 2000 cycles. The submicron spherical structure, with its limited surface area and scarce oxygen-containing moieties, effectively curtails the irreversible sodium-ion loss in solid–electrolyte interphase film formation, resulting in heightened ICE. The abundant nitrogen doping can expand carbon-layer spacing as well as improve the electron/ion-transport dynamics, guaranteeing a high sodium storage capacity and a strong rate capability. Crucially, the synthesis method presented here is straightforward, effective, and amenable to scaling, offering a novel avenue for the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D28673%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHongwei%2BTao%252C%2BSha%2BLi%252C%2BZhijun%2BZhao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D22%26contentID%3Dacsami.4c04795%26title%3DLow-Surface-Area%2BNitrogen-Doped%2BCarbon%2BSubmicrospheres%2Bas%2BHigh-Coulombic-Efficiency%2Band%2BHigh-Capacity%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BPractical%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D28682%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290958&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Adsorption"> Adsorption </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials"> Materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290865&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nitrogen"> Nitrogen </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nitrogen-doped+Carbon+Submicrospheres&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nitrogen-doped%20carbon%20submicrospheres">nitrogen-doped carbon submicrospheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Polymerization+Reaction&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_polymerization%20reaction">polymerization reaction</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Initial+Coulombic+Efficiency&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_initial%20Coulombic%20efficiency">initial Coulombic efficiency</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Capacity&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20capacity">high capacity</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Developing an efficient, clean, and renewable new energy system is the key to solving global energy problems. As a simple and efficient energy storage technology, secondary batteries play a vital role in important application fields of new energy such as photovoltaic engineering, wind power generation, smart grids, and clean transportation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), epitomizing cutting-edge rechargeable battery technology, dominate virtually the entire market of electronic products and are esteemed in key sectors like electric vehicle manufacturing and energy storage facilities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4−8)</a> Nevertheless, lithium resources on earth are severely limited (crustal abundance 0.006%) and unevenly distributed (mainly in South America). Lithium’s resource scarcity and economic considerations are anticipated to emerge as pivotal constraints in the expansive application of energy storage technologies. Sodium, akin to lithium in its group on the periodic table, exhibits similar chemical properties and is abundant (crustal abundance 2.64%) and more cost-effective. Therefore, the construction of a high-performance battery system based on sodium-ion intercalation reaction has more application advantages than LIBs in the realm of large-scale energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 9 to 15">(9−15)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Nevertheless, relative to lithium ions, the augmented radius of sodium ions precipitates diminished Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and a less stable electrode/electrolyte interfacial structure, thereby complicating the selection of viable electrode materials for sodium storage applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 16 to 20">(16−20)</a> Carbon materials, known for their abundant availability, low cost, and straightforward processing methods, have garnered considerable interest among researchers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 21 to 24">(21−24)</a> In particular, hard carbon (HC) materials have emerged as attractive anode candidates, attributed to their varied configurations and favorable redox potentials in sodium-based systems. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25−27)</a> However, traditional HC anodes for sodium storage exhibit diminished initial Coulombic efficiencies (ICEs), compromised sodium storage capacities, and subpar rate performances, due to the constricted carbon interlayer spacing and the lethargic diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. Designing nanostructures is a common approach to elevate the sodium storage efficacy of HC. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28−30)</a> Constructing tailored nanostructures serves to augment the interfacial area between the electrode and electrolyte as well as shorten the diffusion pathway for sodium ions within HC. For instance, Yang et al. introduced a novel type of hard nanofibers, demonstrating a notable capacity of 250 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, along with enhanced cycling stability exceeding 1200 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Nevertheless, these materials often exhibit restricted capacity (&lt;300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and modest ICE (&lt;70%), posing challenges in the construction of practical sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Introducing heteroatom doping emerges as another effective strategy for enhancing the electrochemical performance of HC. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 31 to 38">(31−38)</a> Incorporating heteroatoms such as nitrogen, sulfur, oxygen, phosphorus, fluorine, and boron can effectively induce distortion in hexagonal carbon structures and widen the interlayer spacing within carbon materials. Consequently, this modification reduces the resistance encountered during sodium-ion intercalation and extraction processes in HC materials. Moreover, heterogeneous atoms can change the defect concentration, π–π conjugation structure, and charge distribution of HC materials, thereby enhancing both electron-transport characteristics and chemical reactivity of the carbon framework. Recently, Wang et al. fabricated a nitrogen- and sulfur-codoped carbon via a self-templating synthesis. It exhibits an exceptional sodium storage capacity of 316.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and demonstrates a notable rate performance, delivering 178.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate of 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Nevertheless, the ICE still requires significant enhancement. Hence, there remain formidable obstacles in the pursuit of practical HC electrodes with high ICE, realizable capacities, and stable cyclic retention.</div><div class="NLM_p last">HC, delineated by its structure of curved, disoriented carbon lamellae featuring structural anomalies, inclusive of pentagonal and heptagonal carbon rings, and adorned with oxygenated functional entities such as hydroxyl and carboxyl moieties, experiences an irreversible sodiation process at these defect locales and oxygen-rich functional groups. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39−41)</a> This nonreversible sodiation phenomenon, alongside its pivotal role in solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer genesis, culminates in a diminished ICE within HC anodic materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Therefore, reducing the specific surface area of HC materials and decreasing the oxygen-containing functional groups on the surface are expected to improve the initial efficiency of the electrode. Relative to alternative forms such as nanofibers, nanorods, nanosheets, and core–shell configurations, carbon spheres exhibit a reduced specific surface area. This characteristic diminishes the prevalence of oxygen-containing functional groups on HC surfaces. A lower concentration of these groups curtails the irreversible consumption of sodium ions during SEI formation, thereby enhancing the ICE of HC. In this work, we introduce an ecofriendly, straightforward one-step polymerization technique for synthesizing nitrogen-doped carbon submicrospheres (NCSMs). Leveraging a distinctive structure characterized by minimal surface area, fewer oxygen-rich functional groups, and enhanced nitrogen-doping sites, our synthesized NCSMs exhibit impressive electrochemical performances. These include an exceptional ICE of 84.8%, a remarkable reversible capacity of 384 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, an impressive rate performance, achieving 215 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and unparalleled cycling durability extending beyond 2000 cycles. These characteristics potentially establish the NCSM as an optimal anode candidate for advanced SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The nitrogen-doped carbon submicrospheres (NCSMs) were synthesized by a polymerization reaction at room temperature followed by high-temperature carbonization. Typically, 1.5 mL of NH<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O, 80 mL of ethanol, and 180 mL of distilled water were mixed evenly with vigorous agitation for 0.5 h. Then, 1.0 g of dopamine hydrochloride was introduced to the prior mixture, followed by continuous stirring for 30 h. Following the transition of the solution to a brownish coloration, the resultant precipitates were segregated using centrifugal separation and underwent several rounds of purification with ethanol and deionized water. The obtained samples were then subjected to a vacuum-drying process at 80 °C for 10 h, thereby yielding the desired precursor material. The precursor underwent calcination in an argon environment at 1100 °C for 2 h, facilitating the synthesis of NCSMs. For comparison, the carbon nanospheres without nitrogen doping (CNS) were prepared through the following process. A total of 6 g of glucose was uniformly dissolved in deionized water by vigorous stirring. The prepared solution was transferred to a 100 mL autoclave and subjected to thermal treatment at 180 °C for 4 h. Postcooling, the brown sediment was isolated via centrifugation and underwent successive washes with deionized water and ethanol. The precursor, after vacuum drying, was then heat-treated at 1100 °C for 2 h under an argon shield, culminating in the synthesis of the CNS composite.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The structural and electronic configurations of the samples were elucidated via X-ray diffraction (XRD; PANalytical BV, Holland) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS; model AXIS-ULTRA DLD). Surface area assessments were conducted employing the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller method for nitrogen adsorption–desorption, utilizing a TriStar II 3020 system. Microstructural characterizations were performed through scanning electron microscopy (SEM; type SIRION200) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM; model JEOL2100).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical characterization of the synthesized materials was conducted utilizing CR2025-type coin cells. The working electrode formulation comprised a mixture of the active material, conductive additive Super P, and binder poly(vinylidene fluoride) in a solvent of <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone, following a mass ratio of 7:2:1, with an active material density of 1.8–2.0 mg cm<sup>–3</sup>. Sodium metal of high purity (A.R., &gt;99.7%), procured from Shanghai Aladdin Biochemical Technology Company, Ltd., was fashioned into wafers of 100–200 μm thickness and 12 mm diameter, functioning as both counter and reference electrodes. A Whatman glass fiber served as the separator, and the electrolyte comprised 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in dimethoxyethane. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analyses were executed on a Chenhua electrochemical workstation (Shanghai, China). Galvanostatic cycling assays were undertaken utilizing a LANHE battery testing apparatus (Wuhan, China) within an operational voltage spectrum of 0.005–3.0 V.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Computational Method</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Density functional theory (DFT) analyses were meticulously executed utilizing the <i>Gaussian 09</i>, revision D.01, computational framework. Geometric optimizations were conducted via the M06-2X functional coupled with the 6-31G(d,p) basis set. For each geometrically optimized structure, single-point energy evaluations were performed at the M06-2X/6-31G(d,p) theoretical level. The calculation of adsorption energies (<i>E</i><sub>ad</sub>) adhered to the formula <i>E</i><sub>ad</sub> = <i>E</i><sub>composite</sub> – (<i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> + <i>E</i><sub>graphene</sub>), where <i>E</i><sub>composite</sub> denotes the total energy of the composite system, <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> signifies the energy of the isolated sodium ion, and <i>E</i><sub>graphene</sub> represents the energy of the pristine graphene substrate. Further, the assessment of the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO)/lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy gap, electrostatic potential (ESP), and reduced density gradient were accomplished using the advanced capabilities of the <i>Multiwfn</i> computational tool. Isosurface mapping and visualization tasks were adeptly handled via the Visual Molecular Dynamics (<i>VMD</i>) software, version 1.9.3.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The NCSMs were synthesized via a polymerization reaction at room temperature followed by high-temperature carbonization. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>, flake dopamine hydrochloride (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>) reacts with ammonia to form dopamine (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>). Due to the abundance of −OH and −NH<sub>2</sub> functional groups in dopamine, it readily undergoes self-polymerization, yielding spherical polymerized dopamine precursors (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>). In the terminal annealing phase, the polymer precursors are subjected to carbonization, resulting in the in situ generation of NCSMs (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/medium/am4c04795_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Diagram illustrating the synthetic process of the NCSM.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c04795&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/medium/am4c04795_0011.gif" alt="" id="GRAPHIC-d14e392-autogenerated"></div></div></span><div class="NLM_p">SEM and TEM techniques were employed for the morphological and particle size characterization of the NSCM. The obtained NCSM sample still maintains a uniform spherical structure with a diameter of 750 nm after carbonization at 1100 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a–c). The size of the NSCM sphere is slightly smaller than that of the polymer precursor due to high-temperature decomposition of the organic polymer (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>). The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) of the NCSM reveals an absence of distinct crystal lattices, indicating its amorphous structure. Energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images of a single carbon submicrosphere (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d–f) display a uniform distribution of carbon and nitrogen within the NCSM, attributed to homogeneous nitrogen doping resulting from in situ carbonization of the polydopamine precursor. This uniformity in doping is advantageous for sodium storage due to the combined effects of increased active sites and improved electronic conductivity. In contrast, the CNS was prepared via a dual-step method involving an initial hydrothermal reaction followed by subsequent high-temperature carbonization. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a–c</a>, the CNS sample emerges as well-dispersed nanospheres with a size of 100 nm. The absence of distinct lattices in the HRTEM image of the CNS suggests the amorphous nature of the sample (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3d</a>). Furthermore, the EDS test did not detect the presence of nitrogen, proving the successful preparation of carbon nanospheres without nitrogen doping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/medium/am4c04795_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a and b) SEM, (c) TEM, and (d–f) EDS mapping images of the NCSM.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c04795&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">XRD analysis was conducted to investigate the crystallographic characteristics of both the NCSM and CNS specimens. The XRD patterns, depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, display two pronounced diffraction peaks at approximately 24° and 43°. These peaks are emblematic of the (002) and (100) crystallographic planes, aligning with the typical diffraction patterns associated with amorphous carbon structures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Compared with the CNS, the (002) crystal plane of the NCSM sample exhibits a lower degree shift, suggesting an expanded interlayer spacing <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> in the NCSM. Employing the principles of Bragg’s equation, the calculated interlayer spacings (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) for the CNS and NCSM are deduced to be approximately 0.372 and 0.391 nm, respectively. Nitrogen-atom doping can improve the electrostatic repulsion between carbon layers, resulting in an enlarged carbon-layer spacing of the NCSM samples. The enlarged layer spacing is conducive to the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby helping to increase the plateau capacity of the NCSM sample. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b presents Raman spectra of two samples. Within the Raman spectra of both samples, characteristic peaks manifest at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup>, attributable to the D and G bands, respectively. The D band epitomizes the presence of structural anomalies and disorder in the hexagonal graphitic layers, while the G band pertains to the vibrational modes of sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon atoms within a two-dimensional hexagonal lattice framework. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> A notable observation is the elevated <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> intensity ratio in the NCSM compared to the CNS, which implies that nitrogen doping markedly intensifies the defect density within the carbonic spheroidal structures. XPS analyses were conducted to explore the chemical state and bonding information on the NCSM and CNS. As shown in the XPS survey spectra, compared with the CNS, the NCSM sample contains nitrogen peaks in addition to carbon and oxygen characteristic peaks, further proving the successful doping of nitrogen in the NCSM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). Based on the elemental analysis data, the NCSM manifests a nitrogen content of approximately 3% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). The C 1s XPS spectrum of the NCSM exhibits three salient peaks, located at 284.7, 285.9, and 289.7 eV, which are respectively assignable to the C═C, C–C═N, and COOH groups. Conversely, the C 1s XPS spectrum of the CNS also delineates into three peaks, resonating at 284.5, 285.0, and 288.5 eV, corresponding to the C═C, C–O, and COOH bonding configurations, respectively, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref37" aria-label="reference 33 and 37">(33,37)</a> The ratio of the peak areas can be calculated to quantify the proportions of the three functional groups of the C 1s XPS spectrum. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>, the proportion of oxygen-containing functional groups (C–O and COOH) in the CNS (55.2%) is significantly higher than that in the NCSM (21.5%). In general, the sodiation of oxygen-containing functional groups is irreversible, and it consumes more sodium ions for SEI formation. Therefore, less oxygen-containing functional groups are beneficial to the NCSM in achieving high ICE. The N 1s XPS spectral analysis of the NCSM delineates four principal peaks, identifiable as pyridinic nitrogen (N-6, at 398.0 eV), pyrrolic nitrogen (N-5, at 399.1 eV), graphitic nitrogen (N-Q, at 401.0 eV), and oxidized nitrogen (N-O, at 402.9 eV) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref37" aria-label="reference 33 and 37">(33,37)</a> These nitrogen configurations are quantitatively represented as 17.6% for N-6, 9.6% for N-5, 48.3% for N-Q, and 24.5% for N-O (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>). Pyridinic and pyrrolic nitrogen functionalities are instrumental in facilitating sodium-ion dynamics, thereby enhancing capacitive interactions on the electrode interface. The incorporation of graphitic nitrogen is pivotal in modulating the electron-donating attributes of the carbon lattice, augmenting its electrical conductivity. Additionally, the oxidized nitrogen is vital in augmenting the interfacial wettability between the electrolyte and HC, thereby streamlining the transport kinetics of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref32" aria-label="reference 28 and 32">(28,32)</a> The pore-size distribution and specific surface area of the material were rigorously characterized via nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm analyses. As delineated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f, both the NCSM and CNS manifest type II isotherms, confirming their nonporous architectures. Remarkably, the NCSM exhibits a specific surface area of merely 5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, substantially inferior to that of the CNS, which is quantified at 264 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. The diminished specific surface area and lack of porosity aid in minimizing the irreversible loss of sodium ions during formation of the SEI film, thereby enhancing the ICE of the NCSM specimen.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/medium/am4c04795_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, (c) XPS survey spectra, and (d) C 1s XPS spectra of the NCSM and CNS samples, (e) N 1s XPS spectrum of the NCSM, and (f) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of the NCSM and CNS.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c04795&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">CV analyses were conducted to elucidate the sodium storage behaviors of the NCSM and CNS. Parts a and b of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> exhibit the CV profiles of the NCSM and CNS at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> across a potential range from 0.005 to 3 V. The initial cathodic scans display two major irreversible reduction peaks at 0.95 and 0.45 V, correlating to Na<sup>+</sup> interaction with surface COOH groups and SEI formation, respectively. It is noteworthy to mention that, apart from the two peaks observed at 0.95 and 0.45 V, an additional broad irreversible peak is evident in the CNS sample around 1.1 V, attributed to sodiation of the C–O group. In the subsequent scans, a distinctive pair of redox peaks emerges at 0.01 and 0.10 V, denoting the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes occurring within the graphite microcrystalline layer and nanovoids. Parts c and d of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> depict the charge–discharge profiles of the NCSM and CNS at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> during the initial three cycles. Initially, the NCSM electrode demonstrates a charge capacity of 331.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a discharge capacity of 391.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, achieving a Coulombic efficiency of 84.8% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). The high ICE with inspiring reversible capacity was hardly achieved in any other nitrogen-doping carbon-based materials in previously reported literatures, as presented in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. For comparison, the CNS electrode delivers 186.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of charge capacity and 314.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of discharge capacity with an ICE of 59.3% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). The enhanced ICE observed in the NCSM is ascribed to its unique structural attributes, notably a minimized surface area coupled with a reduced presence of oxygen-containing functional groups, which collectively foster improved sodium storage capabilities. Notably, both samples display distinct charge and discharge profiles characterized by two regions: a plateau region (0.005–0.1 V) and a sloping region (0.1–1.5 V). Typically, the plateau area is associated with Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction within the carbon layer, while the slope area is attributed to the sodium adsorption–desorption on defect sites, surface bonds, and filling pores. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/suppl_file/am4c04795_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, the NCSM sample exhibits a slope capacity comparable to that of the CNS, while demonstrating a significantly higher plateau capacity. This enhanced plateau capacity in the NCSM is attributed to the effective nitrogen doping, which increases the spacing within the carbon layer, consequently facilitating superior sodium insertion/extraction behaviors.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/medium/am4c04795_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a and b) CV and (c and d) charge–discharge curves of the NCSM and CNS electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c04795/asset/images/large/am4c04795_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c04795&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1.
